<div id="review_729959717" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/729959717" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5863991-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451268976p2/5863991.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/729959717?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/5863991-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/31830282-the-sandman-vol-6">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=1990s">1990s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=public-library">public-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=have-reread">have-reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer729959717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3780603805627845427">1/9/2017 - This is my 3rd time reading this collection. I decided to add to the previous review I did last year, since writing an entirely new review for each of the stories seemed like too much work. (Also, I've edited this review so the only spoiler tags are for later volumes; I haven't said anything about major twists in these stories.)<br/><br/>I think this is my favorite of the short story collections, especially for the stories "Thermidor," "Orpheus," and "Ramadan." The 9 stories in this volume all </span>
  <span id="freeText3780603805627845427" style="display:none">1/9/2017 - This is my 3rd time reading this collection. I decided to add to the previous review I did last year, since writing an entirely new review for each of the stories seemed like too much work. (Also, I've edited this review so the only spoiler tags are for later volumes; I haven't said anything about major twists in these stories.)<br><br>I think this is my favorite of the short story collections, especially for the stories "Thermidor," "Orpheus," and "Ramadan." The 9 stories in this volume all focus on a different aspect of history or myth. Gene Wolfe’s introduction gives some background on the historical &amp; mythical figures in each story. This collection was originally published in two arcs: The “Distant Mirrors” arc focuses on political power (in original publication order these are "Thermidor," "August," and "Three Septembers &amp; A January." "Ramadan" was supposed to end this arc but ended up being published after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25105.The_Sandman__Vol__7_Brief_Lives" title="The Sandman, Vol. 7 Brief Lives by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman, Vol. 7: Brief Lives</a>, due to art delays). The other arc, which revolves around storytellers, consists of "Convergences," consists of "The Hunt," "Soft Places," and "The Parliament of Rooks." "Orpheus" is separate. On my 3rd reading of this volume, I’m starting to think that the arcs should have been kept together, and I read them in that order. <br><br>"Three Septembers &amp; A January" - In which Dream makes a bet against Desire, Despair, and Delirium over the fate of a man who has lost all his wealth. Can Dream keep him from entering Despair’s realm? This character, Joshua Abraham Norton, was a real person, who decided that he was the Emperor of the United States of America. He was prescient in calling for a bridge crossing connecting San Francisco to Oakland, and a corresponding tunnel to be built under San Francisco Bay. Both projects were eventually built as the Oakland Bay Bridge and the Transbay Tube. In contrast to the authoritarian governments portrayed in "Thermidor" and "August" Emperor Norton, given the dream of becoming the first and only Emperor of the United States, secures the respect of his people and implements his legacy without repression. This story has one of Sandman's rare happy endings. 4 stars<br><br>This isn’t as dark as a few of the other stories, but it gives the reader a glimpse of the dark side of the Endless as they play games with people’s lives. <br><br>Favorite line: "His madness keeps him sane." <br><br>"Thermidor" - This story is named for the month of July in the short-lived <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Republican_Calendar%E2%80%9D&gt;French%20Revolutionary%20calendar.&lt;/a&gt;%20This%20is%20also%20the%20first%20appearance%20of%20Orpheus%20in%20Sandman.%20He%20was%20referred%20to%20in%20&lt;a%20href=" title="The Sandman, Vol. 3 Dream Country by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman, Vol. 3: Dream Country</a> as the son of Dream and the muse Calliope. (In the original legend, Orpheus’s mother is Calliope but his father is the king of Thrace, <a>Oeagrus.)</a> <br><br>When I first read this I had already read story of Orpheus from Ovid’s Metamorphoses. (So far it’s still the only part of the Metamorphoses that I have read, but I’ll get to it eventually.) In Ovid’s version, Orpheus dies when the Bacchante - aka the Maenads, violent, crazed women in service to Dionysus - tear him apart. So I wasn’t expecting Orpheus to turn up as a still-living severed head. (There is a version of the story in which his head is taken to the island of Naxos. It's referred to in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1846744.The_Argonautica" title="The Argonautica by Apollonius of Rhodes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Argonautica</a>, but the idea of Orpheus’s <i>surviving</i> head is original to Sandman, as far as I know.) <br><br>As the story opens, Orpheus’s head has been stolen from the sanctuary on Naxos. Dream knows where he is, but he won’t get directly involved, so he asks Lady Johanna Constantine to rescue Orpheus for him. She agrees, and finds him in France during the Reign of Terror. Johanna's errand for Dream was mentioned way back in "Men of Good Fortune" from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25099.The_Sandman__Vol__2_The_Doll_s_House" title="The Sandman, Vol. 2 The Doll's House by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman, Vol. 2: The Doll's House</a>.<br><br>I won’t go into the rest of the plot, but this is a story about the power of the imagination. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This is a repeated theme in Sandman, but clearly it can’t solve everything; the story ends with Orpheus, restored to Naxos, asking Johanna to let his father know he misses him. (What happened between them is explained in “Orpheus.”) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> 5 stars <br><br>Favorite lines:<br><br>Johanna asking Dream for help (in a dream, naturally) when she’s imprisoned by the Jacobin government: "Your son’s head is valuable to you, and I am attached to mine. Indeed, hitherto we have been inseparable."<br><br>This exchange, between Robespierre and Johanna:<br>- "The myths are dead. The gods are dead. The ghosts and ghouls and phantoms are dead. There is only the State, and the People."<br>- "No, Monsieur Robespierre. There is much more than that…" <br><br>Johanna trying to comfort Orpheus (she’s quoting Thomas Paine): "What we obtain too cheap we esteem too lightly, Orpheus…"<br><br>The Hunt - This one is about werewolves, and is also a love story and a fable about the value of unfulfilled dreams, told by a grandfather to his skeptical, rational teenage daughter. Her comments are pretty funny. This one is a good use of the unreliable narrator, in ways that only comics can do, with the words telling one story and the panels another. I like this one, but it doesn't have the emotional depth of some of the other stories in this collection. 3 stars<br><br>Favorite line: "You shouldn’t trust the storyteller; only trust the story."<br><br>August - This one, set in ancient Rome, is extremely disturbing. It is a story about revenge, and also about abuses of power. It’s not the best story in the collection, but it’s a memorable one, and has some interesting thematic ties to the rest of the series. The narrator tells of a day he spent with the Emperor Augustus, on the one day a year that Augustus leaves his role as emperor and spends the day as an anonymous beggar. His reasons for doing this are revealed later in the story, but this time around I was struck by Augustus wondering, at the end of the day, whether he was an actor, or whether this was the one day on which he did not have to act. This aspect of the story is a nice parallel with Lucifer’s abdication of Hell in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25101.The_Sandman__Vol__4_Season_of_Mists" title="The Sandman, Vol. 4 Season of Mists by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman, Vol. 4: Season of Mists</a>, and with <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Destruction leaving his realm, and of course with Morpheus's death - as, one after the other, these kings surrender their kingdoms in order to be free of their corresponding obligations. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> 4 stars<br><br>Favorite line: "They follow leaders — queens or kings, chiefs or emperors. We tell them what to do, and they do it. We know no more than they, but still they follow us, blindly, as people lost in the catacombs would follow a child carrying a flaming torch."<br><br>Soft Places - In which Marco Polo gets lost in a sandstorm and crosses into Dream’s realm. This story is unique to this collection, in that part of it takes place in contemporary times and part of it takes place in Marco Polo’s time. I like this one, but it doesn’t really stand out. 3 stars<br><br>Favorite line: "Any view of things that is not strange is false."<br><br>Orpheus - I already knew how this would end when I first read it, and I was surprised that it hit me so hard since I knew the story. Still does. This is a mostly straightforward retelling; it’s really the backstory for later volumes. But it does explain why Orpheus didn't die when the Bacchante attacked him, and provides an explanation for why they attacked him that fits in with Sandman. Besides, I think it adds emotional resonance to later events. 5 stars <br><br>some spoilery reactions:<br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The story of Orpheus was sad to begin with, but rereading it and knowing the repercussions makes me want to tear my hair out. *flails* <br><br>There’s one thing that doesn’t quite make sense to me, though: Dream’s response to Orpheus at the beginning of this issue. "I’m your son. Why won’t you tell me what you know?" "Because you are my son." So, Dream knows what Orpheus’s prophetic dream is about. Later, Destiny refuses to wish Orpheus well at the wedding ("I am Potmos. I do not wish. I know. What must happen will happen") and Dream could have simply said the same thing - that it’s not his place to get involved. But what he says here is slightly different from that. Why? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484000308i/21670636._SY540_.jpg" width="80" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Favorite lines: <br>	- Orpheus to Death: "My other uncles and aunts, Aunt Teleute. I wish they could also have stayed for the party." "They had  things to do, Orpheus. “But you stayed." "I also have things to do, Orpheus." (She has <i>business</i> at the wedding, but he doesn’t know that…)<br>	- <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Destruction’s <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> assessment of Orpheus: "You’re a romantic fool. But that’s no surprise: you get that from your father."<br>	- Orpheus, asking where Death lives: "She has a house?" "She has lots of things, although she seldom has much use for them. You should see her floppy hat collection."<br><br>The Parliament of Rooks: A 'parliament of rooks' is an old name for a group of rooks, just as a group of crows is a 'murder of crows' and a group of ravens is an 'unkindness of ravens.' In this one Eve, Cain and Abel take turns telling stories in the Dreaming. <br><br>Cain’s story is the one referred to in the title. Why <i>do</i> they call it a parliament of rooks? Eve’s story is about Adam’s three wives. (The narration indicates that this story is from the Midrash; I'd come across Lilith before, but not the second wife.) The first was Lilith, expelled from Eden because she would not submit to Adam. The second had no name. Adam saw her being made, and was disgusted; he wouldn’t have anything to do with her. So to replace her, God made Eve from Adam’s rib. <br><br>Abel’s story is about how Cain and Abel came to the Dreaming. It’s adorable, as is Jill Thompson’s art. 4 stars.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484000713i/21670665.jpg" width="40" height="100" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Favorite lines: "You find out that inside someone you know there’s just mucus and meat and blood and bone. They menstruate, salivate, defecate and cry, you know? Sometimes it can just kill the romance."<br><br>"I keep telling you: It's the mystery that endures. Not the explanation. A good mystery can last forever."<br><br>Ramadan - Set in Baghdad during the reign of Haroun al Rashid, who makes a bargain with Dream. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Dream ensures that Baghdad will never be forgotten, but it lives on only in stories and dreams. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1483999501i/21670547._SY540_.jpg" width="40" height="100" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The artwork here is the best in the collection, possibly the best in Sandman. The line art, by P. Craig Russell, is stunningly intricate, and the colors, by colorist Lovern Kindzierski, are amazing too. And the writing is just gorgeous. One of the interesting things about this story is how it contrasts the romanticized elements of a fairytale Baghdad with the more disturbing ones (things like the king’s torture chambers). Dream and Haroun al-Rashid have a great deal in common; both distant and proud kings, quite ruthless when something gets in their way, and unwilling to let go of the past. In fact, Haroun reflects Dream's negative qualities so strongly that Dream comes across as the reasonable one in this tale. Neither of them, though, has the power to stop time. There's an <a href="http://sequart.org/magazine/18668/the-other-egg-of-the-phoenix-understanding-the-50th-issue-of-sandman/" rel="nofollow noopener">insightful essay</a> on this story by Harry Edmundson-Cornell at Sequart. I'm not going to try to summarize it, but it's well worth reading. It’s one my two favorite pieces of critical analysis of Sandman that I've seen so far. I’ll link to the other one in a later review, when it's relevant. <br><br>Incidentally, there was foreshadowing for this issue: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[in vol. 4. Morpheus puts the globe in which he locks up Azazel next to the city-in-a-bottle of Baghdad. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> 5 stars<br><br>Favorite lines: all of them?! Maybe "Ambassadors come here from the ends of the earth to see this miracle; and they return to their kings, saying, we have seen the perfect city, there can be none like it; and their kings are then dissatisfied with their own small fiefs and domains, for they know that never can they compare to to Baghdad, jewel of cities."<br><br>My previous reviews: <br>The Doll’s House: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/729957865">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br>Dream Country: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7299587">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br>Season of Mists: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/725577649">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br>A Game of You: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5863991">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br><br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_306968560" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/306968560" />
    <a title="Ronyell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1169488-ronyell"><img alt="Ronyell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506581787p2/1169488.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/306968560?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ronyell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ronyell" href="/user/show/1169488-ronyell">Ronyell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=graphic-novels-comics">graphic-novels-comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=my-blog-reviews">my-blog-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=fairy-tales-folktales">fairy-tales-folktales</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=russian-books">russian-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=modern-age-comics">modern-age-comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=vertigo-comics">vertigo-comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=strong-hero">strong-hero</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=strong-heroine">strong-heroine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=neil-gaiman">neil-gaiman</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer306968560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9954263316138722865">After reading the fifth volume in Neil Gaiman’s fantastic “Sandman” series, “A Game of You,” I just had to read the sixth volume of the “Sandman” series called “Fables and Reflections.” In this volume, we are introduced to more miscellaneous stories that involve Morpheus and his siblings’ involvement with various characters’ dreams.<br/><br/>Just like the third volume of the “Sandman” series “Dream Country, “ “Fables and Reflections” is mainly a collection of different tales that detail the adventures tha</span>
  <span id="freeText9954263316138722865" style="display:none">After reading the fifth volume in Neil Gaiman’s fantastic “Sandman” series, “A Game of You,” I just had to read the sixth volume of the “Sandman” series called “Fables and Reflections.” In this volume, we are introduced to more miscellaneous stories that involve Morpheus and his siblings’ involvement with various characters’ dreams.<br /><br />Just like the third volume of the “Sandman” series “Dream Country, “ “Fables and Reflections” is mainly a collection of different tales that detail the adventures that Morpheus and his siblings encountered when they are interfering with many people’s dreams. There are a total of nine stories in this volume and they are:<br /><br /><b>
  <u>1) Fear of Falling</u>
</b><br /><br />Morpheus helps a young man overcome his fears of possible failure in his acting career.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>2) Three Septembers and a January</u>
</b><br /><br />Morpheus and his siblings all pitch in to help an old man named Joshua Norton become emperor of the United States of America.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>3) Thermidor</u>
</b><br /><br />Morpheus helps a young woman named Lady Johanna Constantine retrieve the dismembered head of Orpheus in 17th century France.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>4) The Hunt</u>
</b><br /><br />A Grandfather tells his granddaughter an old tale that details the adventures of Vassily and his visit to claim the hand of a beautiful princess.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>5) August</u>
</b><br /><br />This tale relates about how Augustus Caesar receives a special visit from Dream and decides to play the role of a beggar.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>6) Soft Places</u>
</b><br /><br />Marco Polo, famous explorer meets up with Fiddler’s Green and Dream.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>7) Orpheus</u>
</b><br /><br />This relates the dark tale of Orpheus who loses his love after she is killed by a snake and he decides to go down to the underworld to get her back.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>8) The Parliament of Rooks</u>
</b><br /><br />Matthew the Raven, Cain and Abel and Eve all tell twisted versions of certain biblical stories to Morpheus’ baby son, Daniel.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>9) Ramadan</u>
</b><br /><br />This relates the tale of a famous king in Baghdad named Haroun Al Raschid, who wishes to give up his city to Dream.<br /><br />Like the third volume of the “Sandman” series, “Dream Country,” this is basically a collection of miscellaneous tales that detail the adventures that Dream and his siblings have with dealing with other people’s dreams and is not technically apart of the major story that was presented in “Preludes and Nocturnes,” “The Doll’s House,” “Season of Mists” and “A Game of You.” Neil Gaiman never ceases to amaze me with his talented writing and brilliant retelling of classic fairy tales and myths. I always loved the dark and surreal world of Dream and his siblings and it was great seeing them do their duties in the dream world while meddling a bit in the dreams of various people. Probably my favorite stories in this volume were “Thermidor,” “Orpheus” and “The Parliament of Rooks” because they were all mysterious and intense at the same time. What I loved about the story “Thermidor” was how a young woman was coming up with a plan that would save France from the Tyranny it was place on and the idea about a talking decapitated head was truly creepy yet interesting at the same time! I loved “Orpheus” as it was a truly sad tale with a gruesome ending that really made me feel for Orpheus’ loss of his wife. “The Parliament of Rooks” was a truly unusual story as it retold many biblical stories such as “Adam and Eve” and “Cain and Abel” and put a dark twist to those stories. I really enjoyed how Neil Gaiman wove various mythologies (in the story “August”) and fairy tales (in the story “The Hunt”) into the “Sandman” stories and created a dark and interesting world within these stories. Bryan Talbot, Stan Woch, P. Craig Russell, Shawn McManus, John Watkiss, Jill Thompson, Duncan Eagleson, and Kent Williams’ artwork just truly stood out in this volume as they are truly beautiful and frightening to look at, especially of the images of the beautiful world that Orpheus lives in as we see green hillsides and gorgeous cliffs. Since I am a huge fan of gory images, the details being made in the images where characters are beheaded and being torn to shreds was done expertly and I really cringed at the gory images of the characters being killed off in a gruesome fashion.<br /><br />As with the other volumes in the “Sandman” series, this volume contains many gory scenes such as some scenes of rats being smashed and one scene where a person is beheaded and you can see blood spurt everywhere. For readers who do not like reading gory scenes, these scenes might be a bit too uncomfortable for them to read. Also, another problem I had with this volume is that some of the stories lack a bit of action and I often got a little bored with some of these stories and I wished that there was some kind of action going on in some of these stories that would keep me interested in the stories.<br /><br />Overall, “The Sandman: Fables and Reflections” is a great volume that details the random adventures of Dream and his siblings, but it could have been a bit better if there was more action in the stories.<br /><br /><b>Review is also on: <a href="http://rabbitearsbookblog.blogspot.com/2012/04/sandman-fables-and-reflections-volume-6.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Rabbit Ears Book Blog</a></b> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9954263316138722865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1658828799" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1658828799" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1658828799?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18925185-the-sandman-vol-6">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dark-fantasy-gothic">dark-fantasy-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=horror-occult">horror-occult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1658828799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11173240128197485181"><b>Sandman, Vol. 6: Fables and Reflections: Luminous tales of rulers, adventurers, dreamers</b><br/><br/>After Vol 5: A Game of You, my least favorite Sandman volume so far, I’m happy to report a resounding return to form in Vol 6: Fables and Reflections, a collection of stand-alone stories centered on various prominent figures in different periods of history, including the Emperor of the United States in 19th century San Francisco (“Three Septembers and a January”), Robespierre in early 18th century revolutiona</span>
  <span id="freeText11173240128197485181" style="display:none"><b>Sandman, Vol. 6: Fables and Reflections: Luminous tales of rulers, adventurers, dreamers</b><br /><br />After Vol 5: A Game of You, my least favorite Sandman volume so far, I’m happy to report a resounding return to form in Vol 6: Fables and Reflections, a collection of stand-alone stories centered on various prominent figures in different periods of history, including the Emperor of the United States in 19th century San Francisco (“Three Septembers and a January”), Robespierre in early 18th century revolutionary Paris (“Thermidor”), a mysterious huntsman deep in the forest (“The Hunt”), Augustus Caesar in ancient Rome (“August”), Marco Polo roaming in the desert (“Soft Places”), Orpheus and Eurydice in the Underworld (“The Song of Orpheus”), Adam &amp; Eve in the Garden of Eden (“The Parliament of Rooks”), and finally Haroun Al Raschid, Caliph of Bagdad (“Ramadan”).<br /><br />It’s hard to easily summarize the disparate stories of this collection. I found the quality of the narratives and artwork to be very high, but the themes were so rich and varied that it would wrong to say what, as a whole, the collection is “about”. As with all of Gaiman’s SANDMAN series, the art of story-telling itself is a central feature, along with various mythologies, dreamers, powerful rulers, and of course dreams. The way these themes are interwoven is what gives the collection a unified “feel”, even though each story is unique. Frequently there is a framing device, an overt narrating voice, that sets the stage for the stories, but some of my favorite stories keep the identity of the story-teller deliberately concealed in order to be revealed at the end and force us to rethink the story’s overall message. <br /><br />My particular favorites in this collection were “The Hunt”, which has a timeless appeal as a grandfather tells a story to his teenage granddaughter, who would rather watch MTV. The longest story is “The Song of Orpheus”, which takes the traditional tale and incorporates The Endless into the story for a very different interpretation. It is quite a powerful story with a shocking ending. Morpheus has a major role to play here, as do his siblings. <br /><br />“Ramadan” is the centerpiece of the collection, an amazingly illustrated story that is so beautiful to look at that you simply have to read it more than once. The artist’s name is P. Craig Russell, and his drawing of the towers, mosques, minarets, and markets of Bagdad are strongly reminiscent of the art of Moebius. It’s story of the mighty King of fantastic Bagdad, who despite his limitless power and wealth is troubled deep in his soul. It is very much in the vein of The Arabian Nights, but once again Morpheus is a seamless part of the tale, and the ending…ah, that is truly sublime. <br /><br />Interspersed within and between these stories, we continue to learn more about the nature of The Endless, including the missing sibling who makes his first appearance in “The Song of Orpheus”. So in that sense it has more continuity with Vol 4: Season of Mists than Vol 5: A Game of You. One of the best things about SANDMAN is how Gaiman can explore traditional mythology in such fresh and unique ways by overlaying The Endless and their heretofore hidden roles in the stories that humankind has created throughout history. <br /><br />There are so many tantalizing hints dropped by Morpheus and his siblings about their various roles and duties, which again begs the question of “Who made these rules and why?” If The Endless came before the gods, then who created them? Clearly these questions point us to the Creator, who remains behind the scenes even in Vol 4: Season of Mists, when his Angels are they to represent his will. Will he ever make an appearance before this series ends? Only one way to find out…</span>
  <a data-text-id="11173240128197485181" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_831412365" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/831412365" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/831412365?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401231231" href="/book/show/10180611-fables-and-reflections">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer831412365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15495467172777498769">Though the first seems more like a preface than a fully realized story, I was impressed by every one of the nine short stories in this volume, thus the 5 stars. While it's true that each is a standalone, some contain themes that seem to comment (<i>reflect</i>?) on each other; or include recurring characters, such as Orpheus, or even characters from the main storyline, such as Lyta and Johanna Constantine. I discovered another "comment/reflection" as I was, fittingly enough, falling asleep.<br/><br/>I was amused</span>
  <span id="freeText15495467172777498769" style="display:none">Though the first seems more like a preface than a fully realized story, I was impressed by every one of the nine short stories in this volume, thus the 5 stars. While it's true that each is a standalone, some contain themes that seem to comment (<i>reflect</i>?) on each other; or include recurring characters, such as Orpheus, or even characters from the main storyline, such as Lyta and Johanna Constantine. I discovered another "comment/reflection" as I was, fittingly enough, falling asleep.<br /><br />I was amused by Olethros telling Orpheus that Teleute (Death) <i>has lots of things, although she seldom has much use for them. You should see her floppy hat collection.</i> and by the images of Abel's <i>sanitized pablum</i> (as Cain calls it) of a story. Though I anticipated the ending of the last story (dealing with Baghdad), it was nevertheless poignant, pointing out the power of a story's mystery in an otherwise despairing world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15495467172777498769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1130680093" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11951948-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569447207p2/11951948.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130680093?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/11951948-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401231231" href="/book/show/10180611-fables-and-reflections">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11951948-paul?shelf=books-read-2015">books-read-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1130680093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5713686127375549340">In this sixth book of the Sandman series, Gaiman takes a brief pause from the main storyline. There are nine of new stories in here, some short, others longer, with characters from earlier stories and a host of new ones.<br/> <br/> Compared to earlier books, this is not as dark or bleak, and Gaiman has dusted the stories within with a touch of whimsy, but they still have depth and the ability to make you think. Some are based in America, but he travels time and the world in the tales, with Morpheus appear</span>
  <span id="freeText5713686127375549340" style="display:none">In this sixth book of the Sandman series, Gaiman takes a brief pause from the main storyline. There are nine of new stories in here, some short, others longer, with characters from earlier stories and a host of new ones.<br /> <br /> Compared to earlier books, this is not as dark or bleak, and Gaiman has dusted the stories within with a touch of whimsy, but they still have depth and the ability to make you think. Some are based in America, but he travels time and the world in the tales, with Morpheus appearing in some of them.<br /><br />Having read some of the others, I didn’t feel that it was as strong, but he picks up on the dreams of the characters all the way through the stories. They are still intense and richly illustrated, and that it what makes these such a pleasure to read. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5713686127375549340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3532394348?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James DeSantis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James DeSantis" href="/user/show/16060275-james-desantis">James DeSantis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401231231" href="/book/show/10180611-fables-and-reflections">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3532394348" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17223934764682478275">This goes back to more standalone stories. <br/><br/>Which means we're going to having varying quality as you read through the book. <br/><br/>There's some great ones in here like Fear of Falling, which is a nice short story about someone not wanting to fail, but too scared of trying. I liked this one a lot. August is great tale of Ceaser's nephew, and a dark past, but we all knew this was coming. I also really enjoyed the main tale here of The Song of Orpheus which is probably the highlight to see Dream's son and </span>
  <span id="freeText17223934764682478275" style="display:none">This goes back to more standalone stories. <br /><br />Which means we're going to having varying quality as you read through the book. <br /><br />There's some great ones in here like Fear of Falling, which is a nice short story about someone not wanting to fail, but too scared of trying. I liked this one a lot. August is great tale of Ceaser's nephew, and a dark past, but we all knew this was coming. I also really enjoyed the main tale here of The Song of Orpheus which is probably the highlight to see Dream's son and his downfall. You know it's going to be sad but I wasn't prepared for how sad. <br /><br />There's a few stories that miss the mark for me. especially the last one. Not a fan of the story, the structure, or the art. Kind of downer for me overall. I also didn't love the Marco Polo story. <br /><br />But overall it had a lot more good than bad, and that's what I've come to expect of Sandman. A 4 out of 5.</span>

<div id="review_484243681" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243681" />
    <a title="Tanya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya"><img alt="Tanya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602837639p2/15698555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/484243681?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanya" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya">Tanya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401231231" href="/book/show/10180611-fables-and-reflections">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484243681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7217337292985938171"><blockquote>
  <i>"It is sometimes a mistake to climb; it is always a mistake never even to make the attempt. If you do not climb you will not fall. This is true. But is it that bad to fail, that hard to fall? (...) Sometimes the fall kills you. And sometimes, when you fall, you fly."</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>After <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243626' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Dream Country</i>
</a>, this is another collection of short one-issue stories. The ones collected in <i>Fables & Reflections</i> were originally published over a period of three years, and similarly to Volume Three, the stories don't d</span>
  <span id="freeText7217337292985938171" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>"It is sometimes a mistake to climb; it is always a mistake never even to make the attempt. If you do not climb you will not fall. This is true. But is it that bad to fail, that hard to fall? (...) Sometimes the fall kills you. And sometimes, when you fall, you fly."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />After <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243626" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Dream Country</i>
</a>, this is another collection of short one-issue stories. The ones collected in <i>Fables &amp; Reflections</i> were originally published over a period of three years, and similarly to Volume Three, the stories don't directly contribute to the main story arc (<i>The Song of Orpheus</i> being the one notable exception), but rather provide additional subtext that helps in understanding Morpheus' motivations.<br /><br />Despite these stories all being separate entities, they are part of two shorter story arcs that share certain themes and structures: The four stories from the <i>Distant Mirrors</i> arc all take their titles from months, and are about emperors and power (<i>Three Septembers and a January</i>, <i>Thermidor</i>, <i>August</i>, and <i>Ramadan</i>), while the three stories from the <i>Convergence</i> arc are structured as stories within stories (<i>The Hunt</i>, <i>Soft Places</i>, and <i>The Parliament of Rooks</i>). The collection mostly alternates between the two arcs rather than presenting them chronologically, and I think that the shuffling was a good choice, and adds to a feeling of variety (although it makes no sense to have placed <i>Thermidor</i> before <i>The Song of Orpheus</i>). I personally find the stories included in the second half to be stronger than those in the first half, the exact opposite of <i>Dream Country</i>.<br /><br /><i>Fear of Falling</i> · ★★★<br />This is a very short story from a Vertigo promotional comic, about a playwright/director who, on the eve of a rehearsal for his upcoming Off-Broadway play, decides to leave it all behind out of fear of the consequences, whether they be success or failure. He has his mind made up until Morpheus appears in a dream...<br /><br /><i>Three Septembers and a January</i> · ★★★<br />A story concerning the mostly true story of Joshua Abraham Norton, first, last, and only Emperor of the United States of America, framed as the result of a challenge between Dream and three of his siblings—Despair, Delirium, and Desire.<br /><br /><i>Thermidor</i> · ★★½<br />Dream asks Lady Johanna Constantine, who briefly appeared in the <i>Men of Good Fortune</i> interlude in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243593" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Doll's House</i>
</a> volume, to help retrieve the severed head of his son Orpheus from France, amid the chaos of the French Revolution, featuring real historical characters.<br /><br /><i>The Hunt</i> · ★★★½<br />In modern-day New Jersey, a grandfather tells his reluctant granddaughter a fairy tale from their people in the Old Country—a story in the Slavic tradition, involving werewolves and beings from Eastern European and Russian folklore, such as Baba Yaga and Koschei.<br /><br /><i>August</i> · ★★★★<br />The Roman Emperor Augustus spends one day a year as a beggar. While he talks with a dwarf actor who helps him with the disguise, we learn of his life, and how he chose the future of the Roman Empire from two sets of prophecies... with it being implied that he uses this one day a year to plot the fall of the Empire after his death, because Morpheus told him that the gods could not spy on his thoughts that way.<br /><br /><i>Soft Places</i> · ★★★<br />A young Marco Polo gets lost in the desert, at a location where the boundary between reality and the Dreaming blurs. He encounters Rustichello da Pisa, with whom he'd later co-write his autobiography, Fiddler's Green, and finally Morpheus, who has just escaped the captivity detailed in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243577" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Preludes &amp; Nocturnes</i>
</a>, and helps him find his way back to his caravan.<br /><br /><i>The Song of Orpheus</i> · ★★★★<br />A retelling of the Greek myth of Orpheus—in this Universe, the son of Morpheus and Calliope, as has been established in <i>Dream Country</i>—involving the Endless. Death and Destruction aid him with instructions on how to reach the Underworld to plead for his lost bride Eurydice, while Morpheus refuses to help, and is thus estranged from both his wife and son. Contrary to the myth, after being dismembered by the Maenads, Orpheus' decapitated head is kept alive indefinitely (hence its appearance in the story <i>Thermidor</i>, set centuries later, as well as the upcoming <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243691" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Brief Lives</i>
</a>).<br /><br /><i>The Parliament of Rooks</i> · ★★★★<br />Daniel, child of Hippolyta Hall, who had appearances in both Volumes Two and Four, is now a toddler, and visits the Dreaming during his afternoon nap. He gets picked up by Matthew and Eve, and they go to visit Cain and Abel in their Houses of Secrets and Mysteries together, where turns are taken to tell stories—the natural phenomenon of the "parliament of rooks", the three wives of Adam, and how Cain and Abel came to live in the Dreaming.<br /><br /><i>Ramadan</i> · ★★★★★<br />The Caliph of the glittering and glorious Baghdad during the Islamic Golden Age is troubled by the impermanence of such perfection, and calls upon Morpheus to preserve it forever. This story with its many hints and references to <i>One Thousand and One Nights</i> is my absolute favorite one-shot of the whole <i>Sandman</i> run—the sobering ending gives me goosebumps every time, and the Middle Eastern influenced art and lettering are absolutely stunning.<br /><br />—————<br /><br />My other ડꪖꪀᦔꪑꪖꪀ reviews:<br /><br />01: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243577" rel="nofollow noopener">Preludes &amp; Nocturnes</a> · ★★★½<br />02: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243593" rel="nofollow noopener">The Doll's House</a> · ★★★★<br />03: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243626" rel="nofollow noopener">Dream Country</a> · ★★★<br />04: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243647" rel="nofollow noopener">Season of Mists</a> · ★★★★½<br />05: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243656" rel="nofollow noopener">A Game of You</a> · ★★★½<br />06: Fables and Reflections · ★★★½<br />07: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243691" rel="nofollow noopener">Brief Lives</a> · ★★★★½<br />08: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243742" rel="nofollow noopener">Worlds' End</a> · ★★★<br />09: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243798" rel="nofollow noopener">The Kindly Ones</a> · ★★★★★<br />10: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243796" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wake</a> · ★★★<br /><br /><a <div id="review_2909716308" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2909716308" />
    <a title="Zachary F." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22548315-zachary-f"><img alt="Zachary F." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593017877p2/22548315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2909716308?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zachary F." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zachary F." href="/user/show/22548315-zachary-f">Zachary F.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=historical-fic">historical-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=to-love-the-unreal">to-love-the-unreal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=things-in-heaven-and-earth">things-in-heaven-and-earth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=1950-to-1999">1950-to-1999</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2909716308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4685263135032762319"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1566858675i/28055474._SX540_.jpg' width='362.5' height='261' alt='from Three Septembers and a January' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Like the earlier <i>Sandman</i> collection <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2901248091' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Dream Country</i>
</a>, this one is a grab bag of standalone stories only tenuously connected to one another. Unlike that collection, <i>Fables</i> has nine tales instead of four, all of them originally published in between other story arcs or in one-off specialty issues.<br/><br/>Given these stories' disparate nature (and the fact that I read them in their original publication order, rather than all lumped together), it's hard to give a unified review of the whole thing. Even the </span>
  <span id="freeText4685263135032762319" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1566858675i/28055474._SX540_.jpg" width="362.5" height="261" alt="from Three Septembers and a January" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Like the earlier <i>Sandman</i> collection <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2901248091" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Dream Country</i>
</a>, this one is a grab bag of standalone stories only tenuously connected to one another. Unlike that collection, <i>Fables</i> has nine tales instead of four, all of them originally published in between other story arcs or in one-off specialty issues.<br><br>Given these stories' disparate nature (and the fact that I read them in their original publication order, rather than all lumped together), it's hard to give a unified review of the whole thing. Even the artwork isn't consistent, since somewhere around this point the <i>Sandman</i> team ceased to be anything like a permanent group and instead began trading out artists almost issue-to-issue. But none of that is a bad thing per se, and in fact I think the thematic and visual variety here provides a nice refreshing spritzer just when the series is starting to get a little stale. (Ahem, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2915937298" rel="nofollow noopener">A Game of You</a>
</i>...)<br><br>All but a couple of these stories are rooted deeply in history or mythology, from ancient Greece and Rome to 8th-century Baghdad to Robespierre's France to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emperor_Norton" rel="nofollow noopener">Emperor Norton</a>'s San Francisco, and (as I've remarked more than once during this read-through of the series) I think that's the sort of material Gaiman handles best. Most of them at least held my interest, and there were one or two I’d count among the series' best, along with a few I could live without.<br><br>A perfectly pleasant read, all things considered, but after six of these collections I'm running out of new things to say about them. <i>Sandman</i> fans'll like it, and that's who'll be reading it anyway.<br><br>----<br>See my reviews for the whole <i>Sandman</i> series here:<br><br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2892445761" rel="nofollow noopener">Preludes and Nocturnes</a>
</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2893930178" rel="nofollow noopener">The Doll's House</a>
</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2901248091" rel="nofollow noopener">Dream Country</a>
</i> (★★★☆☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2905131601" rel="nofollow noopener">Season of Mists</a>
</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2915937298" rel="nofollow noopener">A Game of You</a>
</i> (★★★☆☆)<br><i>Fables and Reflections</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2924339790" rel="nofollow noopener">Brief Lives</a>
</i> (★★★☆☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2927938325" rel="nofollow noopener">World's End</a>
</i> (★★☆☆☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3238072957" rel="nofollow noopener">The Kindly Ones</a>
</i> (★★☆☆☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3415474289" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wake</a>
</i> (★★★☆☆)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4685263135032762319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7145345435574978209">Like volume 3 this part also consists of short stories from several different authors where Sandman himself is more or less secondary character.<br/>Stories in this collection where lot better than those in volume 3, which I thought was low point of the series.<br/>It's definitively not as good as volume 2 or 4 but still worthy read for fans of series.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rNnl9vuQ98cAhVk3cv/g10atu4tMUcSdXKbgWLQ+TFNtJqwfGcUuh2V4BNtiHAsgQAcxfl5ExWSFKiarqAGbXw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZdqJ9+SzJFr1x0+LlJrw8h5X0svJ3QuAIvGZnt7j6eykJcAeBub9GpA6EmeEeRsFGP1YPtvICnn7fV9twtw+4A==')})
  }
//]]>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_23529'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23529.From_Hell?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">From Hell<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3961.Alan_Moore?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Alan Moore<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.17 avg rating &mdash; 33,407 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1999\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer1171594305800254737\">\"I shall tell you where we are. We\'re in the most extreme and utter region of the human mind. A dim, subconscious underworld. A radiant abyss where men meet themselves. Hell, Netley. We\'re in Hell.\" \n\nHaving proved himself peerless in the arena of re<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1171594305800254737\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;I shall tell you where we are. We\'re in the most extreme and utter region of the human mind. A dim, subconscious underworld. A radiant abyss where men meet themselves. Hell, Netley. We\'re in Hell.&quot; \n\nHaving proved himself peerless in the arena of reinterpreting superheroes, Alan Moore turned his ever-incisive eye to the squalid, enigmatic world of Jack the Ripper and the Whitechapel murders of 1888. Weighing in at 576 pages, From Hell is certainly the most epic of Moore\'s works and remarkably and is possibly his finest effort yet in a career punctuated by such glorious highlights as Watchmen and V for Vendetta. Going beyond the myriad existing theories, which range from the sublime to the ridiculous, Moore presents an ingenious take on the slaughter. His Ripper\'s brutal activities are the epicentre of a conspiracy involving the very heart of the British Establishment, including the Freemasons and The Royal Family. A popular claim, which is transformed through Moore\'s exquisite and thoroughly gripping vision, of the Ripper crimes being the womb from which the 20th century, so enmeshed in the celebrity culture of violence, received its shocking, visceral birth. \n\nBolstered by meticulous research that encompasses a wide spectrum of Ripper studies and myths and coupled with his ability to evoke sympathies in such monstrous characters, Moore has created perhaps the finest examination of the Ripper legacy, observing far beyond society\'s obsessive need to expose Evil\'s visage. Ultimately, as Moore observes, Jack\'s identity and his actions are inconsequential to the manner in which society embraced the Fear: &quot;It\'s about us. It\'s about our minds and how they dance. Jack mirrors our hysterias. Faceless, he is the receptacle for each new social panic.&quot; \n\nEddie Campbell\'s stunning black and white artwork, replete with a scratchy, dirty sheen, is perfectly matched to the often-unshakeable intensity of Moore\'s writing. Between them, each murder is rendered in horrifying detail, providing the book\'s most unnerving scenes, made more so in uncomfortable, yet lyrical moments as when the villain embraces an eviscerated corpse, craving understanding; pleading that they &quot;are wed in legend, inextricable within eternity&quot;. \n\nThough technically a comic, the term hardly begins to describe From Hell\'s inimitable grandeur and finesse, as it takes the medium to fresh heights of ingenuity and craftsmanship. Moore and Campbell\'s autopsy on the emaciated corpse of the Ripper myth has divulged a deeply disturbing yet undeniably captivating masterpiece. —Danny Graydon<\/span>\n  <a This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
